{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_013wvb3hM6UrSxREMMqZZux3","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"f03a4a12-dc55-4f8b-8ea5-fea5802347c4","uuid":"1ef7d924-6af6-4c2e-a2fd-11b1f4b71716","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md"}}}

For each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release, the major version (first digit)\\n47: would remain `0`.\\n48: \\n49: - Bugfixes will result in the patch version (third digit) changing. PRs that are\\n50: cherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\\n51: to the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\\n52: changing the patch version.\\n53: \\n54: #### Branches and tags.\\n55: \\n56: We will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\\n57: number. We will create only a new tag for each increment in the patch\\n58: version number. See [semver](http://semver.org/) for definitions of major,\\n59: minor, and patch.\\n60: \\n61: The HEAD of the master branch in client-go will track the HEAD of the master\\n62: branch in the main Kubernetes repo.\\n63: \\n64: #### Compatibility: your code <-> client-go\\n65: \\n66: The `v0.x.y` tags indicate that go APIs may change in incompatible ways in\\n67: different versions.\\n68: \\n69: See [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md) for guidelines on requiring a specific\\n70: version of client-go.\\n71: \\n72: #### Compatibility: client-go <-> Kubernetes clusters\\n73: \\n74: Since Kubernetes is backwards compatible with clients, older `client-go`\\n75: versions will work with many different Kubernetes cluster versions.\\n76: \\n77: We will backport bugfixes--but not new features--into older versions of\\n78: `client-go`.\\n79: \\n80: \\n81: #### Compatibility matrix\\n82: \\n83: |                               | Kubernetes 1.29 | Kubernetes 1.30 | Kubernetes 1.31 | Kubernetes 1.32 | Kubernetes 1.33 | Kubernetes 1.34 |\\n84: | ----------------------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- | --------------- |\\n85: | `kubernetes-1.29.0`/`v0.29.0` | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\\n86: | `kubernetes-1.30.0`/`v0.30.0` | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\\n87: | `kubernetes-1.31.0`/`v0.31.0` | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              | +-              |\\n88: | `kubernetes-1.32.0`/`v0.32.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              | +-              |\\n89: | `kubernetes-1.33.0`/`v0.33.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               | +-              |\\n90: | `kubernetes-1.34.0`/`v0.34.0` | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | ✓               |\\n91: | `HEAD`                        | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              | +-              |\\n92: \\n93: Key:\\n94: \\n95: * `✓` Exactly the same features / API objects in both client-go and the Kubernetes\\n96:   version.\\n97: * `+` client-go has features or API objects that may not be present in the\\n98:   Kubernetes cluster, either due to that client-go has additional new API, or\\n99:   that the server has removed old API. However, everything they have in\\n100:   common (i.e., most APIs) will work. Please note that alpha APIs may vanish or\\n101:   change significantly in a single release.\\n102: * `-` The Kubernetes cluster has features the client-go library can't use,\\n103:   either due to the server has additional new API, or that client-go has\\n104:   removed old API. However, everything they share in common (i.e., most APIs)\\n105:   will work.\\n106: \\n107: See the [CHANGELOG](./CHANGELOG.md) for a detailed description of changes\\n108: between client-go versions.\\n109: \\n110: | Branch         | Canonical source code location      | Maintenance status |\\n111: | -------------- | ----------------------------------- | ------------------ |\\n112: | `release-1.25` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.25 branch   | =-                 |\\n113: | `release-1.26` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.26 branch   | =-                 |\\n114: | `release-1.27` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.27 branch   | =-                 |\\n115: | `release-1.28` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.28 branch   | =-                 |\\n116: | `release-1.29` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.29 branch   | =-                 |\\n117: | `release-1.30` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.30 branch   | =-                 |\\n118: | `release-1.31` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.31 branch   | =                  |\\n119: | `release-1.32` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.32 branch   | ✓                  |\\n120: | `release-1.33` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.33 branch   | ✓                  |\\n121: | `release-1.34` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.34 branch   | ✓                  |\\n122: | client-go HEAD | Kubernetes main repo, master branch | ✓                  |\\n123: \\n124: Key:\\n125: \\n126: * `✓` Changes in main Kubernetes repo are actively published to client-go by a bot\\n127: * `=` Maintenance is manual, only severe security bugs will be patched.\\n128: * `-` Deprecated; please upgrade.\\n129: \\n130: #### Deprecation policy\\n131: \\n132: We will maintain branches for at least six months after their first stable tag\\n133: is cut. (E.g., the clock for the release-2.0 branch started ticking when we\\n134: tagged v2.0.0, not when we made the first alpha.) This policy applies to\\n135: every version greater than or equal to 2.0.\\n136: \\n137: #### Why do the 1.4 and 1.5 branch contain top-level folder named after the version?\\n138: \\n139: For the initial release of client-go, we thought it would be easiest to keep\\n140: separate directories for each minor version. That soon proved to be a mistake.\\n141: We are keeping the top-level folders in the 1.4 and 1.5 branches so that\\n142: existing users won't be broken.\\n143: \\n144: ### Kubernetes tags\\n145: \\n146: This repository is still a mirror of\\n147: [k8s.io/kubernetes/staging/src/client-go](https://github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes/tree/master/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go),\\n148: the code development is still done in the staging area.\\n149: \\n150: Since Kubernetes `v1.8.0`, when syncing the code from the staging area,\\n151: we also sync the Kubernetes version tags to client-go, prefixed with\\n152: `kubernetes-`. From Kubernetes `v1.17.0`, we also create matching semver\\n153: `v0.x.y` tags for each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release.\\n154: \\n155: For example, if you check out the `kubernetes-1.17.0` or the `v0.17.0` tag in\\n156: client-go, the code you get is exactly the same as if you check out the `v1.17.0`\\n157: tag in Kubernetes, and change directory to `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go`.\\n158: \\n159: The purpose is to let users quickly find matching commits among published repos,\\n160: like [sample-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-apiserver),\\n161: [apiextension-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/apiextensions-apiserver),\\n162: etc. The Kubernetes version tag does NOT claim any backwards compatibility\\n163: guarantees for client-go. Please check the [semantic versions](#versioning) if\\n164: you care about backwards compatibility.\\n165: \\n166: ### How to get it\\n167: \\n168: To get the latest version, use go1.16+ and fetch using the `go get` command. For example:\\n169: \\n170: ```\\n171: go get k8s.io/client-go@latest\\n172: ```\\n173: \\n174: To get a specific version, use go1.11+ and fetch the desired version using the `go get` command. For example:\\n175: \\n176: ```\\n177: go get k8s.io/client-go@v0.20.4\\n178: ```\\n179: \\n180: See [INSTALL.md](/INSTALL.md) for detailed instructions and troubleshooting.\\n181: \\n182: ### How to use it\\n183: \\n184: If your application runs in a Pod in the cluster, please refer to the\\n185: in-cluster [example](examples/in-cluster-client-configuration), otherwise please\\n186: refer to the out-of-cluster [example](examples/out-of-cluster-client-configuration).\\n187: \\n188: ### Dependency management\\n189: \\n190: For details on how to correctly use a dependency management for installing client-go, please see [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md).\\n191: \\n192: ### Contributing code\\n193: Please send pull requests against the client packages in the Kubernetes main [repository](https://github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes). PRs that are\\n50: cherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\\n51: to the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\\n52: changing the patch version.\\n53: \\n54: #### Branches and tags.\\n55: \\n56: We will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\\n57: number. An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\\n44: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\\n45: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\\n46: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n47: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n48: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n49: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n50: //\\n51: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n52: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n53: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n54: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n55: //\\n56: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n57: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n58: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n59: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n60: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n61: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n62: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n63: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n64: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n65: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n66: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n67: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n68: // selector.\\n69: //\\n70: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n71: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n72: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n73: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n74: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n75: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n76: //\\n77: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n78: // during `Run()`.\\n79: //\\n80: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n81: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n82: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n83: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n84: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n85: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n86: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n87: //\\n88: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n89: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n90: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n91: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n92: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n93: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n94: //\\n95: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n96: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n97: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n98: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n99: // into its constituent parts.\\n100: //\\n101: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n102: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n103: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n104: //\\n105: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\\n106: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\\n107: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\\n108: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\\n109: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\\n110: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\\n111: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\\n112: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\\n113: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\\n114: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\\n115: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\\n116: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n119: // delivered sequentially. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01G1DiSyDRj2agG3x3atky5z","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: # client-go under the hood\\n2: \\n3: The [client-go](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/) library contains various mechanisms that you can use when\\n4: developing your custom controllers. These mechanisms are defined in the\\n5: [tools/cache folder](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/tree/master/tools/cache) of the library.\\n6: \\n7: Here is a pictorial representation showing how the various components in\\n8: the client-go library work and their interaction points with the custom\\n9: controller code that you will write.\\n10: \\n11: <p align=\\\"center\\\">\\n12:   <img src=\\\"images/client-go-controller-interaction.jpeg\\\" height=\\\"600\\\" width=\\\"700\\\"/>\\n13: </p>\\n14: \\n15: ## client-go components\\n16: \\n17: * Reflector: A reflector, which is defined in [type *Reflector* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/reflector.go),\\n18: watches the Kubernetes API for the specified resource type (kind).\\n19: The function in which this is done is *ListAndWatch*.\\n20: The watch could be for an in-built resource or it could be for a custom resource.\\n21: When the reflector receives notification about existence of new\\n22: resource instance through the watch API, it gets the newly created object\\n23: using the corresponding listing API and puts it in the Delta Fifo queue\\n24: inside the *watchHandler* function.\\n25: \\n26: \\n27: * Informer: An informer defined in the [base controller inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/controller.go) pops objects from the Delta Fifo queue.\\n28: The function in which this is done is *processLoop*. The job of this base controller\\n29: is to save the object for later retrieval, and to invoke our controller passing it the object.\\n30: \\n31: * Indexer: An indexer provides indexing functionality over objects.\\n32: It is defined in [type *Indexer* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/index.go). A typical indexing use-case is to create an index based on object labels. Indexer can\\n33: maintain indexes based on several indexing functions.\\n34: Indexer uses a thread-safe data store to store objects and their keys.\\n35: There is a default function named *MetaNamespaceKeyFunc* defined in [type *Store* inside package *cache*](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/tools/cache/store.go)\\n36: that generates an object’s key as `<namespace>/<name>` combination for that object.\\n37: \\n38: \\n39: ## Custom Controller components\\n40: \\n41: * Informer reference: This is the reference to the Informer instance that knows\\n42: how to work with your custom resource objects. Your custom controller code needs\\n43: to create the appropriate Informer.\\n44: \\n45: * Indexer reference: This is the reference to the Indexer instance that knows\\n46: how to work with your custom resource objects. Your custom controller code needs\\n47: to create this. You will be using this reference for retrieving objects for\\n48: later processing.\\n49: \\n50: The base controller in client-go provides the *NewIndexerInformer* function to create Informer and Indexer.\\n51: In your code you can either [directly invoke this function](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/examples/workqueue/main.go#L174) or [use factory methods for creating an informer.](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-controller/blob/master/main.go#L61)\\n52: \\n53: * Resource Event Handlers: These are the callback functions which will be called by\\n54: the Informer when it wants to deliver an object to your controller. The typical\\n55: pattern to write these functions is to obtain the dispatched object’s key\\n56: and enqueue that key in a work queue for further processing.\\n57: \\n58: * Work queue: This is the queue that you create in your controller code to decouple\\n59: delivery of an object from its processing. Resource event handler functions are written\\n60: to extract the delivered object’s key and add that to the work queue.\\n61: \\n62: * Process Item: This is the function that you create in your code which processes items\\n63: from the work queue. There can be one or more other functions that do the actual processing.\\n64: These functions will typically use the [Indexer reference](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/blob/master/examples/workqueue/main.go#L73), or a Listing wrapper to retrieve the object corresponding to the key.\",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/docs/controller-client-go.md\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"f03a4a12-dc55-4f8b-8ea5-fea5802347c4","uuid":"3674c794-82a9-4b9b-84ec-f4e0c48c1604","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: # client-go under the hood\\n2: \\n3: The [client-go](https://github.com/kubernetes/client-go/) library contains various mechanisms that you can use when\\n4: developing your custom controllers.
